the Big Steppers">Mr. Morale &amp; the Big Steppers</a></i>, was released on May 13.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Ahead of its release, he visited <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> to shoot a mini documentary with <a href="/wiki/Spotify" title="Spotify">Spotify</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> He also invited and met music executives and other Ghanaian artists such as <a href="/wiki/Stonebwoy" title="Stonebwoy">Stonebwoy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amaarae" title="Amaarae">Amaarae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_Sherif" title="Black Sherif">Black Sherif</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Smallgod" title="Smallgod">Smallgod</a> during a private album listening party in <a href="/wiki/Accra" title="Accra">Accra</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> In June, he performed at <a href="/wiki/Louis_Vuitton" title="Louis Vuitton">Louis Vuitton</a>'s Men's Spring/Summer 2023 show, in honor of their late artistic director <a href="/wiki/Virgil_Abloh" title="Virgil Abloh">Virgil Abloh</a>, inside the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a> during <a href="/wiki/Paris_Fashion_Week" title="Paris Fashion Week">Paris Fashion Week</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> He later headlined the final night of the <a href="/wiki/Glastonbury_Festival" title="Glastonbury Festival">Glastonbury Festival</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On May 19, 2023, Lamar released a surprise remix of <a href="/wiki/Beyonc%C3%A9" title="Beyoncé">Beyonce's</a> song "AMERICA HAS A PROBLEM" from her Grammy-winning 2022 album, <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_(Beyonc%C3%A9_album)" title="Renaissance (Beyoncé album)"><i>RENAISSANCE</i></a>. This collaboration marks their third project together, following the release of "<a href="/wiki/Freedom_(Beyonc%C3%A9_song)" title="Freedom (Beyoncé song)">Freedom</a>" in 2016 and their appearance on the soundtrack for the 2019 <a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King_(2019_film)" title="The Lion King (2019 film)">live-action Lion King</a> film with the song "<a href="/wiki/The_Nile_(Kendrick_Lamar_and_Beyonc%C3%A9_song)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Nile (Kendrick Lamar and Beyoncé song)">NILE</a>". Notably, this remix represents Lamar's first release of 2023 under his new record label, <a href="/wiki/PGLang" title="PGLang">pgLang</a>, co-founded with <a href="/wiki/Dave_Free" title="Dave Free">Dave Free</a>
</p><p>On May 30, 2023, Lamar and rapper <a href="/wiki/Baby_Keem" title="Baby Keem">Baby Keem</a> released a collaboration song titled "<a href="/wiki/The_Hillbillies" title="The Hillbillies">The Hillbillies</a>". The song features an upbeat, Jersey club-inspired sample of <a href="/wiki/Bon_Iver" title="Bon Iver">Bon Iver’s</a> “<a href="/wiki/Bon_Iver_discography#Singles" title="Bon Iver discography">PDLIF</a>” and was unexpectedly released on the <a href="/wiki/PGLang" title="PGLang">pgLang</a> YouTube channel. The accompanying music video, directed by Neal Farmer, includes a cameo appearance by rapper <a href="/wiki/Tyler,_the_Creator" title="Tyler, the Creator">Tyler, the Creator</a>. The song became available on streaming platforms on June 5, 2023
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artistry">Artistry</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span></h3>
<p>Lamar cites <a href="/wiki/Tupac_Shakur" title="Tupac Shakur">Tupac Shakur</a> as his biggest influence, noting that he has impacted his music and everyday life.<sup id="cite_ref-hiphopdx1_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hiphopdx1-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> He has stated that Shakur, <a href="/wiki/The_Notorious_B.I.G." title="The Notorious B.I.G.">the Notorious B.I.G.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jay-Z" title="Jay-Z">Jay-Z</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nas" title="Nas">Nas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eminem" title="Eminem">Eminem</a> are his top five favorite rappers.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> In a 2011 interview with <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i>, Lamar mentioned <a href="/wiki/Mos_Def" title="Mos Def">Mos Def</a> and <a href="/wiki/Snoop_Dogg" title="Snoop Dogg">Snoop Dogg</a> as rappers that he listened to and took influence from during his early years.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> He also cited <a href="/wiki/DMX" title="DMX">DMX</a> as someone who "really [got me started] on music. "That first album [<i><a href="/wiki/It%27s_Dark_and_Hell_Is_Hot" title="It&#39;s Dark and Hell Is Hot">It's Dark and Hell Is Hot</a></i>] is classic, [so he had an influence on me]." He further added that <a href="/wiki/Eazy-E" title="Eazy-E">Eazy-E</a> also influenced him, saying, "I wouldn't be here today if it wasn't for Eazy-E."<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a September 2012 interview, Lamar stated that Eminem "influenced a lot of my style" and has credited him for his own aggression on records such as "<a href="/wiki/Backseat_Freestyle" title="Backseat Freestyle">Backseat Freestyle</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> Lamar also gave <a href="/wiki/Lil_Wayne" title="Lil Wayne">Lil Wayne</a>'s work in <a href="/wiki/Hot_Boys" title="Hot Boys">Hot Boys</a> credit for influencing his style and praised his longevity.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> He has said that he also grew up listening to <a href="/wiki/Rakim" title="Rakim">Rakim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dr._Dre" title="Dr. Dre">Dr. Dre</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tha_Dogg_Pound" title="Tha Dogg Pound">Tha Dogg Pound</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> In January 2013, when asked to name three rappers that have played a role in his style, Lamar said: "It's probably more of a west coast influence. A little bit of <a href="/wiki/Kurupt" title="Kurupt">Kurupt</a>, [Tupac], with some of the content of <a href="/wiki/Ice_Cube_(rapper)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice Cube (rapper)">Ice Cube</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> In a November 2013 interview with <i>GQ</i>, when asked who are the four MCs that made him, he answered Shakur, Dr. Dre, <a href="/wiki/Snoop_Dogg" title="Snoop Dogg">Snoop Dogg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mobb_Deep" title="Mobb Deep">Mobb Deep</a>, namely <a href="/wiki/Prodigy_(rapper)" title="Prodigy (rapper)">Prodigy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Lamar professed to having been influenced by jazz trumpeter <a href="/wiki/Miles_Davis" title="Miles Davis">Miles Davis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parliament-Funkadelic" title="Parliament-Funkadelic">Parliament-Funkadelic</a> during the recording of <i>To Pimp a Butterfly</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-rapup_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rapup-204">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_style_and_rapping_technique">Musical style and rapping technique</span></h3>
<p>Lamar's musical style is known to be "anti-flamboyant, interior and complex."<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_Review_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_Review-205">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> His work is generally categorized as <a href="/wiki/Progressive_rap" title="Progressive rap">progressive rap</a> due to his contributions of broadening the genre's audience to reach <a href="/wiki/Mainstream_media" title="Mainstream media">mainstream</a> listeners.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> He's stated in an interview that "You really can't categorize my music, it's human music."<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> Lamar's debut studio album <i><a href="/wiki/Section.80" title="Section.80">Section.80</a></i> was categorized as <a href="/wiki/Political_hip_hop#Conscious_hip_hop" title="Political hip hop">conscious hip hop</a>. His second album <i><a href="/wiki/Good_Kid,_M.A.A.D_City" title="Good Kid, M.A.A.D City">Good Kid, M.A.A.D City</a></i> was heavily influenced by <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_hip_hop" title="West Coast hip hop">West Coast hip hop</a> and 1990s <a href="/wiki/Gangsta_rap" title="Gangsta rap">gangsta rap</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> His next three albums, <i><a href="/wiki/To_Pimp_a_Butterfly" title="To Pimp a Butterfly">To Pimp a Butterfly</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Damn_(Kendrick_Lamar_album)" title="Damn (Kendrick Lamar album)">Damn</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Mr._Morale_%26_the_Big_Steppers" title="Mr. Morale &amp; the Big Steppers">Mr. Morale &amp; the Big Steppers</a></i>, are all classified as conscious hip hop records; with <i>To Pimp a Butterfly</i> incorporating elements of <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soul_music" title="Soul music">soul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Funk" title="Funk">funk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spoken_word" title="Spoken word">spoken word poetry</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> <i>Damn</i> drawing on <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_R%26B" title="Contemporary R&amp;B">R&amp;B</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_soul" title="Psychedelic soul">psychedelic soul</a> influences,<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> and <i>Mr. Morale &amp; the Big Steppers</i> utilizing <a href="/wiki/Minimal_music" title="Minimal music">minimalist</a> production.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Labeled as a technical rapper by <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_Review_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_Review-205">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Lamar's "limber and dexterous" <a href="/wiki/Rapping#Flow" title="Rapping">flow</a> switches from <a href="/wiki/Derivative_work" title="Derivative work">derivative</a> to <a href="/wiki/Generative_metrics" title="Generative metrics">generative metrics</a> while incorporating <a href="/wiki/Internal_rhyme" title="Internal rhyme">internal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Multisyllabic_rhymes" title="Multisyllabic rhymes">multisyllabic rhyme</a> schemes.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> He's also known for his "willingness to experiment with his voice," often using <a href="/wiki/Cadence_(poetry)" title="Cadence (poetry)">cadences</a> to convey complex emotions and tell stories through multiple <a href="/wiki/Persona" title="Persona">personas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Tirhakah Love of <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a> writes that by expanding his <a href="/wiki/Vocal_range" title="Vocal range">vocal range</a>, Lamar "calls back to a lineage that runs through <a href="/wiki/James_Brown" title="James Brown">James Brown</a>'s foundational work in the '60s; the <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">psychedelic</a> '70s; <a href="/wiki/Prince_(musician)" title="Prince (musician)">Prince</a>'s sweaty <a href="/wiki/Phantasmagoria" title="Phantasmagoria">phantasmagoria</a> in the '80s; and gangsta rap's run in the '90s. In each of these eras, artists stretched the human voice in order to challenge listeners to consider the forces residing in our bodies, awaiting a sound to call them to life."<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Songwriting">Songwriting</span></h3>
<p>Lamar's <a href="/wiki/Introspection" title="Introspection">introspective</a> and <a href="/wiki/Confessional_writing" title="Confessional writing">confessional</a> songwriting is often based on his personal life.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> He has been branded a "master of storytelling" by <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i>, while his lyricism has been described by <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> as "<a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespearean</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Considered a "radio-friendly but overtly political rapper" by <i><a href="/wiki/Pitchfork_(website)" title="Pitchfork (website)">Pitchfork</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> Lamar's writing often contain <a href="/wiki/Political_criticism" title="Political criticism">political</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_commentary" title="Social commentary">social commentary</a> centered around <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African American</a> life and <a href="/wiki/African-American_culture" title="African-American culture">culture</a>, exploring themes of <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a>, black empowerment and <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social injustice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-225">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> It has been compared to a <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union" title="State of the Union">State of the Union</a> address by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Jacob Uitti of <i><a href="/wiki/American_Songwriter" title="American Songwriter">American Songwriter</a></i> notes, "For as much as he is a musician and a lyricist and an emcee, Lamar is also a playwright, a novelist, a short story author. He's literary within the art form of music."<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>All of Lamar's studio albums have been classified as <a href="/wiki/Concept_album" title="Concept album">concept albums</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> <i>Section.80</i> is themed around <a href="/wiki/Millennials" title="Millennials">Generation Y</a> and dwells on related subjects such as the 1980s <a href="/wiki/Crack_epidemic_in_the_United_States" title="Crack epidemic in the United States">crack epidemic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drug_tolerance" title="Drug tolerance">medication tolerance</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Ronald_Reagan" title="Presidency of Ronald Reagan">presidency of Ronald Reagan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> <i>Good Kid, M.A.A.D City</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Nonlinear_narrative" title="Nonlinear narrative">nonlinear narrative</a> that chronicles Lamar's harsh teenage experiences in his native Compton.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> <i>To Pimp a Butterfly</i> combines political and personal themes such as <a href="/wiki/Social_inequality" title="Social inequality">racial inequality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Institutional_discrimination" title="Institutional discrimination">institutional discrimination</a> to document the Black American experience.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> <i>Damn</i> explores the dualities of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a> and delves into themes of <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spirituality</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> <i>Mr. Morale &amp; the Big Steppers</i> details Lamar's experiences in <a href="/wiki/Psychotherapy" title="Psychotherapy">therapy</a> and explores personal themes such as <a href="/wiki/Childhood_trauma" title="Childhood trauma">childhood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transgenerational_trauma" title="Transgenerational trauma">generational trauma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Accountability" title="Accountability">accountability</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celebrity_worship_syndrome" title="Celebrity worship syndrome">celebrity worship</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lyrics">Lyrics</span></h3>
<p>Lamar's 2015 song "<a href="/wiki/The_Blacker_the_Berry_(song)" title="The Blacker the Berry (song)">The Blacker the Berry</a>" gathered controversy following the lines, "So why did I weep when <a href="/wiki/Trayvon_Martin" title="Trayvon Martin">Trayvon Martin</a> was in the street, when gang-banging make me kill a nigga blacker than me? Hypocrite!" Some fans perceived the line to be Lamar judging the black community.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> Lamar later explained the motivation behind the lyric in an <a href="/wiki/NPR" title="NPR">NPR</a> interview, saying, "It's not me pointing at my community; it's me pointing at myself, I don't talk about these things if I haven't lived them, and I've hurt people in my life. It's something I still have to think about when I sleep at night."<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While Lamar's 2022 song "<a href="/wiki/Auntie_Diaries" title="Auntie Diaries">Auntie Diaries</a>" was met with praise from critics and transgender listeners,<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> the song was also met with heavy criticism due to Lamar's <a href="/wiki/Epizeuxis" title="Epizeuxis">epizeuxis</a> usage of "<a href="/wiki/Faggot_(slang)" class="mw-redirect" title="Faggot (slang)">faggot</a>", as well as <a href="/wiki/Deadnaming" title="Deadnaming">deadnaming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Misgendering" class="mw-redirect" title="Misgendering">misgendering</a> his transgender relative to express his own personal growth from childhood misunderstanding.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feuds">Feuds</span></h3>
<p>In August 2013, Lamar was featured on the song "<a href="/wiki/Control_(Big_Sean_song)" title="Control (Big Sean song)">Control</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Big_Sean" title="Big Sean">Big Sean</a>. In his verse, Lamar called out several rappers, telling them he was going to murder his competition. The verse gathered responses and diss tracks from artists such as <a href="/wiki/Joe_Budden" title="Joe Budden">Joe Budden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Papoose_(rapper)" title="Papoose (rapper)">Papoose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meek_Mill" title="Meek Mill">Meek Mill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sean_Combs" title="Sean Combs">Diddy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lupe_Fiasco" title="Lupe Fiasco">Lupe Fiasco</a>, and <a href="/wiki/B.o.B" title="B.o.B">B.o.B</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> <i>Rolling Stone</i> called the single "one of the most important hip-hop songs of the last decade".<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lamar has been reported to be in a feud with <a href="/wiki/Drake_(musician)" title="Drake (musician)">Drake</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Complex_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Complex (magazine)">Complex</a></i> called their relationship "complicated",<sup id="cite_ref-:02_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-250">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Genius_(website)" class="mw-redirect" title="Genius (website)">Genius</a> called it a "subliminal war",<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/GQ" title="GQ">GQ</a></i> called it a "cold war" due to the mass popularity of both artists.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> Before Lamar's "Control" verse, Lamar had been featured on Drake's "Buried Alive Interlude", Drake was featured on Lamar's single "<a href="/wiki/Poetic_Justice_(song)" title="Poetic Justice (song)">Poetic Justice</a>", and both were featured on <a href="/wiki/A$AP_Rocky" class="mw-redirect" title="A$AP Rocky">A$AP Rocky</a>'s song "<a href="/wiki/Fuckin%27_Problems" title="Fuckin&#39; Problems">Fuckin' Problems</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-:02_250-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-250">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> Drake responded to Lamar's "Control" verse in an interview with <i>Billboard</i>, saying, "I know good and well that Kendrick's not murdering me, at all, in any platform."<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2013, Drake's third album, <i><a href="/wiki/Nothing_was_the_Same" class="mw-redirect" title="Nothing was the Same">Nothing was the Same</a></i>, was released.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> Publications such as <i>Complex</i> speculated that Drake had directed subliminal insults at Lamar in the song "The Language".<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> In an interview with <i>Pitchfork</i> a day later, Drake showed disapproval of "Control", saying he wasn't impressed with it and added, "Mind you, it'll go on– <i>Complex</i> and Rap Radar will give it like, verse of the millennium and all that shit or whatever." Drake later said his only competition was <a href="/wiki/Kanye_West" title="Kanye West">Kanye West</a>, after being asked about Kendrick saying he was murdering his competition.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> Lamar further escalated tensions in the <a href="/wiki/2013_Bet_Hip_Hop_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="2013 Bet Hip Hop Awards">2013 BET Hip-Hop Awards</a> cypher when he referred to Drake during the cypher,<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> saying, "Yeah, and nothing's been the same since they dropped 'Control' / And tucked a sensitive rapper back in his pajama clothes."<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stereogum" title="Stereogum">Stereogum</a> noted that Lamar was referencing Drake's third studio album, <i>Nothing was the Same</i>, and also Drake being called overly sensitive by the media.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2013, Drake, whilst being interviewed by <i>Vice</i>, said he "stood his ground" and he has to realize "I'm being baited and I'm not gonna fall," then refusing to deny that the line on "The Language" was directed to Lamar, saying he "doesn't want to get into responses". Drake later went on to say that he acknowledged the lines in Lamar's cypher were for him and that it wasn't enough for him to prepare a response before saying they haven't seen each other since the BET cypher.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> Several more reported subliminal lines were spoken by each rapper, four by Kendrick on the songs, "Pay for it", "King Kunta", "Darkside/Gone", and "Deep Water" and two by Drake on the songs "Used To" and "4PM In Calabasas".<sup id="cite_ref-:02_250-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-250">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2016, former NFL player and broadcast show host <a href="/wiki/Marcellus_Wiley" title="Marcellus Wiley">Marcellus Wiley</a> alleged that on his ESPN show, Drake or Lamar had given an interview in which they started "talking noise" and claimed that they had problems with the other individual. The interview was eventually not aired and Wiley said it had been "destroyed".<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> Wiley said that the interview would have escalated the reported feud to become official with diss tracks being directed at the other side.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> Following an almost year-long hiatus from music, Lamar released "<a href="/wiki/The_Heart_Part_4" title="The Heart Part 4">The Heart Part 4</a>". It was speculated that Lamar's line, "One, two, three, four, five / I am the greatest rapper alive" was a response to Drake's line, "I know I said top five, but I'm top two / And I'm not two and I got one" on the song "Gyalchester".<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> Kendrick proceeded to insult rappers who have <a href="/wiki/Ghostwriter" title="Ghostwriter">ghostwriters</a> in an interview with <i>Rolling Stone</i> in August 2015. It was speculated that the insult was directed towards Drake, who has seen controversy due to the use of "ghostwriters" on songs such as "<a href="/wiki/R.I.C.O._(song)" title="R.I.C.O. (song)">RICO</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lamar has also feuded with Detroit rapper and former collaborator <a href="/wiki/Big_Sean" title="Big Sean">Big Sean</a>. Following the release of Sean's track "Control" in August 2013 where Lamar calls Sean out and claims he's gonna "murder" him, Sean responded in praise, saying, "Alright, that's what it need to get back to, it need to get back to hip-hop, that culture."<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> In January 2015, Sean later spoke on "Control", saying that the song was "negative"<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> and a month later released the "Me, Myself, and I".<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> In October 2016, Big Sean released "No More Interviews" with shots directed at Lamar.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spotify">Spotify</span></h3>
<p>In May 2018, Anthony "Top Dawg" Tiffith, <a href="/wiki/CEO" class="mw-redirect" title="CEO">CEO</a> of Top Dawg Entertainment, contacted <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Ek" title="Daniel Ek">Daniel Ek</a>, CEO of <a href="/wiki/Spotify" title="Spotify">Spotify</a>, and the streaming service's head of artist relations <a href="/wiki/Troy_Carter_(talent_manager)" title="Troy Carter (talent manager)">Troy Carter</a> to express his frustrations with their <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Spotify#2017–18:_Hate_Content_&amp;_Hateful_Conduct_policy" title="Criticism of Spotify">Hate Content &amp; Hateful Conduct</a> policy.<sup id="cite_ref-TD_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TD-272">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> Conceived in light of the <a href="/wiki/MeToo_movement" title="MeToo movement">#MeToo movement</a>, the removal policy sought to promote "openness, diversity, tolerance and respect" by removing content that promotes, advocates, or incites hatred and violence against an individual or group based on characteristics.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> In accordance with the policy, the streaming platform removed <a href="/wiki/XXXTentacion" title="XXXTentacion">XXXTentacion</a> and <a href="/wiki/R._Kelly" title="R. Kelly">R. Kelly</a>'s music from their editorial and algorithmic playlists due to their publicized acts of violence against women.<sup id="cite_ref-Coscarelli_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coscarelli-276">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> Tiffith claimed that the policy was <a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship">censorship</a> and threatened to pull music from his label, including Lamar's, from the platform if they kept the policy as it stood.<sup id="cite_ref-Snapes_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snapes-277">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> In the midst of Tiffith's threat, Lamar praised XXXTentacion's debut studio album <i><a href="/wiki/17_(XXXTentacion_album)" title="17 (XXXTentacion album)">17</a></i> on Twitter.<sup id="cite_ref-Snapes_277-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snapes-277">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> In response to the criticism, Spotify reversed their policy and reinstated XXXTentacion's music back onto playlists after other artists threatened to pull their music.<sup id="cite_ref-Snapes_277-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snapes-277">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_ventures">Other ventures</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acting_and_filmmaking">Acting and filmmaking</span></h3>
<p>Lamar began his filmmaking career by writing the music video for his song "Ignorance Is Bliss".<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> He made his directorial debut with the music video for "<a href="/wiki/Backseat_Freestyle" title="Backseat Freestyle">Backseat Freestyle</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> Since then, he has directed several of his music videos alongside his creative partner <a href="/wiki/Dave_Free" title="Dave Free">Dave Free</a> under the collective name The Little Homies.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, Lamar produced and was featured in <i>M.A.A.D</i>, a short film inspired by his second studio album <i><a href="/wiki/Good_Kid,_M.A.A.D_City" title="Good Kid, M.A.A.D City">Good Kid, M.A.A.D City</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> In December 2015, he co-directed, edited and starred in the short film <i>God Is Gangsta</i>, which was inspired by his third studio album <i><a href="/wiki/To_Pimp_a_Butterfly" title="To Pimp a Butterfly">To Pimp a Butterfly</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> He began executive producing music videos in 2020 with <a href="/wiki/Baby_Keem" title="Baby Keem">Baby Keem</a>'s "Hooligan".<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> In January 2022, Lamar and Free announced that they will be producing an untitled comedy film with <i><a href="/wiki/South_Park" title="South Park">South Park</a></i> creators <a href="/wiki/Matt_Stone" title="Matt Stone">Matt Stone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trey_Parker" title="Trey Parker">Trey Parker</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2018, Lamar made his acting debut in the fifth season of the <a href="/wiki/Starz" title="Starz">Starz</a> drama series <i><a href="/wiki/Power_(TV_series)" title="Power (TV series)">Power</a></i>, portraying a Dominican drug addict named Laces.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> His casting stemmed from his friendship with rapper 50 Cent, who stars in and executive produces the series.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> Lamar wanted to portray a character that did not resemble his musical persona, and drew inspiration from various people he knew when growing up in Compton. He also compared his acting preparation to his songwriting, saying that he prefers to "always have that open space to evolve".<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> Lamar's performance was praised by critics and viewers, and earned him an <a href="/wiki/NAACP_Image_Awards" title="NAACP Image Awards">NAACP Image Award</a> nomination for Outstanding Guest Performance in a Comedy or Drama Series.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_and_fashion">Business and fashion</span></h3>
<p>Lamar owns a percentage of his former record label Top Dawg Entertainment.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> In December 2014, he was announced as a <a href="/wiki/Brand_ambassador" title="Brand ambassador">brand ambassador</a> for sportswear brand <a href="/wiki/Reebok" title="Reebok">Reebok</a>, and designed six sneakers with the company.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> After ending his ambassadorship with Reebok in 2017, Lamar began a partnership with <a href="/wiki/Nike,_Inc." title="Nike, Inc.">Nike</a> and collaborated on five sneakers.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> In 2022, he collaborated with <a href="/wiki/Converse_(lifestyle_wear)" class="mw-redirect" title="Converse (lifestyle wear)">Converse</a> on two sneakers.<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact">Impact</span></h2>
<p>Lamar's work has earned universal acclaim from music critics, industry peers, and cultural figures. He has been named by numerous media outlets as one of the most influential artists of the <a href="/wiki/2010s_in_music" title="2010s in music">2010s</a>, with <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> listing him as one of the greatest rappers of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> He has been credited by <a href="/wiki/NPR_Music" title="NPR Music">NPR Music</a> with the revival of <a href="/wiki/Jazz_rap" title="Jazz rap">jazz rap</a>, which waned after the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> Writing for <i><a href="/wiki/Esquire_(UK_Edition)" title="Esquire (UK Edition)">Esquire UK</a></i>, editor Olivia Ovended opined, "Even if you're not overly familiar with Lamar's back catalog, his influence in music is everywhere, from the West Coast hip-hop now being made by <a href="/wiki/Anderson_.Paak" title="Anderson .Paak">Anderson .Paak</a>. to the trap of <a href="/wiki/Gucci_Mane" title="Gucci Mane">Gucci Mane</a>. He is—and we'll brook no argument here—the greatest rapper making music today."<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i> journalist Carrie Battan considered him "California's biggest hip-hop artist since <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music#1990s" title="Hip hop music">the nineteen-nineties</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> Lamar has been branded as the "New King of Hip Hop" numerous times by media outlets.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_225-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-225">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:4_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-305">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:5_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-306">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> Steve Batlin of <i>Forbes</i> writes that although he "may or may not be the greatest rapper alive right now," he's "certainly in the very short lists of artists in the conversation."<sup id="cite_ref-:3_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-307">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His music was also used during protests following the <a href="/wiki/2016_United_States_presidential_election" title="2016 United States presidential election">2016 United States presidential election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> According to American studies and media scholar William Hoynes, Lamar's <a href="/wiki/Progressive_rap" title="Progressive rap">progressive rap</a> music places him in a long line of African-American artists and activists who "worked both inside and outside of the mainstream to advance a counterculture that opposes the racist stereotypes being propagated in white-owned media and culture".<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/History_(American_TV_network)" title="History (American TV network)">History.com</a> considered Lamar's Pulitzer Prize win as "a sign of the American cultural elite's recognition of hip-hop as a legitimate artistic medium."<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> <i>NPR Music</i> writer Marcus J. Moore noticed Lamar's rap-jazz aesthetic present on his repertoire, "he's at the vanguard of this movement, proving that he too is a rule breaker, just like <a href="/wiki/Miles_Davis" title="Miles Davis">Miles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbie_Hancock" title="Herbie Hancock">Herbie</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Coltrane" title="John Coltrane">Coltrane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Glasper" title="Robert Glasper">Glasper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roy_Hargrove" title="Roy Hargrove">Hargove</a>, who all took bold creative risks to push <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a> into uncharted territory," and stated that Lamar is introducing jazz to a generation "who might only know it through their parents' old record collections."<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Esquire_(magazine)" title="Esquire (magazine)">Esquire US</a></i> writer Matt Miller opined about the rapper's videography in 2017, crediting Lamar for "reviving" the music video as "a powerful form" of <a href="/wiki/Social_commentary" title="Social commentary">social commentary</a>, citing examples like "Alright" and "Humble".<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Multiple artists have cited his work as an inspiration, including <a href="/wiki/Khalid_(singer)" title="Khalid (singer)">Khalid</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roddy_Ricch" title="Roddy Ricch">Roddy Ricch</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christine_and_the_Queens" title="Christine and the Queens">Christine and the Queens</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jhen%C3%A9_Aiko" title="Jhené Aiko">Jhené Aiko</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/YBN_Cordae" class="mw-redirect" title="YBN Cordae">Cordae</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Car_Seat_Headrest" title="Car Seat Headrest">Car Seat Headrest</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Dua_Lipa" title="Dua Lipa">Dua Lipa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pharrell_Williams" title="Pharrell Williams">Pharrell Williams</a> called Lamar "one of the greatest writers of our times", explaining that he "knows how to be very disciplined with a subject matter, he knows that stickiness is important, and he knows that it has to feel great."<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lorde" title="Lorde">Lorde</a> described him as "the most popular and influential artist in modern music".<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Achievements">Achievements</span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_awards_and_nominations_received_by_Kendrick_Lamar" title="List of awards and nominations received by Kendrick Lamar">List of awards and nominations received by Kendrick Lamar</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:KENDRICK_LAMAR_Grandoozy_2018_(44346900965).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/KENDRICK_LAMAR_Grandoozy_2018_%2844346900965%29.jpg/220px-KENDRICK_LAMAR_Grandoozy_2018_%2844346900965%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/KENDRICK_LAMAR_Grandoozy_2018_%2844346900965%29.jpg/330px-KENDRICK_LAMAR_Grandoozy_2018_%2844346900965%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/KENDRICK_LAMAR_Grandoozy_2018_%2844346900965%29.jpg/440px-KENDRICK_LAMAR_Grandoozy_2018_%2844346900965%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="511" /></a><figcaption>Lamar performing at Grandoozy in September 2018</figcaption></figure>
<p>Throughout his career, Lamar has won 17 <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Awards" title="Grammy Awards">Grammy Awards</a> (including three for <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Rap_Album" title="Grammy Award for Best Rap Album">Best Rap Album</a> and six for <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Rap_Performance" title="Grammy Award for Best Rap Performance">Best Rap Performance</a>—the most won by an artist),<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup> an <a href="/wiki/Emmy_Awards" title="Emmy Awards">Emmy Award</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> four <a href="/wiki/American_Music_Awards" title="American Music Awards">American Music Awards</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> 25 <a href="/wiki/BET_Hip_Hop_Awards" title="BET Hip Hop Awards">BET Hip Hop Awards</a> (the most won by an artist),<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> 11 <a href="/wiki/MTV_Video_Music_Awards" title="MTV Video Music Awards">MTV Video Music Awards</a> (including two <a href="/wiki/MTV_Video_Music_Award_for_Video_of_the_Year" title="MTV Video Music Award for Video of the Year">Video of the Year</a> wins),<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup> 6 <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Music_Awards" title="Billboard Music Awards"><i>Billboard</i> Music Awards</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Brit_Awards" title="Brit Awards">Brit Award</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> As a songwriter, he has received nominations for an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Award</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Awards" title="Golden Globe Awards">Golden Globe Award</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/58th_Annual_Grammy_Awards" title="58th Annual Grammy Awards">58th Annual Grammy Awards</a>, Lamar received the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_milestones" title="Grammy Award milestones">most Grammy nominations by a rapper in one night</a>, with 11.<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-grammys16noms_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grammys16noms-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/65th_Annual_Grammy_Awards" title="65th Annual Grammy Awards">65th ceremony</a>, he became the first artist from any genre to be nominated for <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Album_of_the_Year" title="Grammy Award for Album of the Year">Album of the Year</a> with four consecutive lead studio albums since <a href="/wiki/Billy_Joel" title="Billy Joel">Billy Joel</a> (1979-1983).<sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Good Kid, M.A.A.D City</i>, <i>To Pimp a Butterfly</i>, and <i>Damn</i> were featured in <i>Rolling Stone</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s industry-voted ranking of the <a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone%27s_500_Greatest_Albums_of_All_Time" title="Rolling Stone&#39;s 500 Greatest Albums of All Time">500 greatest albums of all time</a>, as well as their ranking for the 200 greatest hip hop albums of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup> <i>Good Kid, M.A.A.D City</i> was additionally featured in the magazine's list of the 100 best debut albums of all time, and was named the greatest concept album of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup> <i>To Pimp a Butterfly</i> was ranked by several publications as one of the greatest albums of the 2010s decade.<sup id="cite_ref-decade_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decade-337">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup> <i>Damn</i> won the <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize_for_Music" title="Pulitzer Prize for Music">Pulitzer Prize for Music</a> in 2018, marking the first musical composition outside of the classical and jazz genres to receive the honor.<sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup> Lamar's <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Steppers_Tour" title="The Big Steppers Tour">Big Steppers Tour</a> (2022–2023) is the highest-grossing rap tour of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lamar has appeared in various power listings. <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> included him on its annual list of the <a href="/wiki/Time_100" title="Time 100">100 most influential people in the world</a> in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-time_100_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_100-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He has appeared on <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i>' <a href="/wiki/Forbes_Celebrity_100" title="Forbes Celebrity 100">Celebrity 100</a> ranking (2019),<sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup> and its <a href="/wiki/Forbes_30_Under_30" title="Forbes 30 Under 30">30 Under 30</a> list twice in the music category (2014 and 2018).<sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup> Lamar was included twice in <i>Billboard</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s lists of the greatest rappers of all time (2015 and 2023).<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup> <i>Complex</i> named him the best rapper alive twice (2013 and 2017),<sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> and included him in their list of the 20 best rappers in their 20s thrice (2013, 2015, and 2016).<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On May 11, 2015, Lamar was honored with the <a href="/wiki/California_State_Senate" title="California State Senate">California State Senate</a>'s Generational Icon Award by state senator <a href="/wiki/Isadore_Hall_III" title="Isadore Hall III">Isadore Hall III</a> for his contributions to music and his community.<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup> He was later honored as the <a href="/wiki/Grand_marshal" title="Grand marshal">Grand Marshal</a> of the 63rd annual Compton Christmas Parade.<sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup> On February 13, 2016, he was presented with the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Keys_to_the_City_in_the_United_States" title="List of Keys to the City in the United States">key to the city</a> of Compton by mayor <a href="/wiki/Aja_Brown" title="Aja Brown">Aja Brown</a> for "representing Compton's evolution" and "embodying the new vision for Compton." Brown also added that Lamar serves as a "catalyst to raise a new level of consciousness for this generation. His message challenges the status quo and motivates listeners to rethink our society's institutions."<sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationships_and_family">Relationships and family</span></h3>
<p>Lamar is an older cousin of fellow rapper <a href="/wiki/Baby_Keem" title="Baby Keem">Baby Keem</a>, and a younger cousin of basketball player <a href="/wiki/Nick_Young_(basketball)" title="Nick Young (basketball)">Nick Young</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-352" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-352">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-353" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-353">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> He is a notable fan of the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Lakers" title="Los Angeles Lakers">Los Angeles Lakers</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Dodgers" title="Los Angeles Dodgers">Los Angeles Dodgers</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Rams" title="Los Angeles Rams">Los Angeles Rams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-354" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-354">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup> While attending Centennial High School, Lamar began dating beautician Whitney Alford, who was a classmate of his.<sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup> Their relationship was strained due to his <a href="/wiki/Sexual_addiction" title="Sexual addiction">sex addiction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Infidelity" title="Infidelity">infidelity</a>; which caused them to break up in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup> During their separation, he dated emcee <a href="/wiki/Nitty_Scott" title="Nitty Scott">Nitty Scott</a> for approximately one year.<sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup> They amicably split following growing concerns from her management team at the time, who felt that it was "not a good look".<sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After <i>Good Kid, M.A.A.D City</i> (2012) was released, Lamar and Alford rekindled their relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup> During an April 2015 interview with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Breakfast_Club_(radio_show)" title="The Breakfast Club (radio show)">The Breakfast Club</a></i>, Lamar revealed that they were engaged.<sup id="cite_ref-engagement_363-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-engagement-363">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup> In July 2019, the couple welcomed their first child, a daughter named Uzi.<sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup> Alford contributed background vocals on select songs from <i>To Pimp a Butterfly</i> (2015), and is the primary narrator on <i>Mr. Morale &amp; the Big Steppers</i> (2022).<sup id="cite_ref-366" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-366">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup> Through the cover art of the latter album, the couple revealed that they welcomed their second child, a son named Enoch.<sup id="cite_ref-368" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-368">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs">Beliefs</span></h3>
<p>Lamar is a devout <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christian</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-370" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-370">&#91;369&#93;</a></sup> having <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Christianity" title="Conversion to Christianity">converted</a> as a teenager following the death of a friend.<sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">&#91;370&#93;</a></sup> He seldom attended church services as a child, but felt "spiritually unsatisfied" due to the empty and "kinda one-sided" nature of the <a href="/wiki/Sermon" title="Sermon">sermons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-372" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-372">&#91;371&#93;</a></sup> He has been <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptized</a> twice: first when he was 16 and again in October 2013, which he announced during his opening set at <a href="/wiki/Kanye_West" title="Kanye West">Kanye West</a>'s <a href="/wiki/The_Yeezus_Tour" title="The Yeezus Tour">Yeezus Tour</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_373-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-373">&#91;372&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-374" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-374">&#91;373&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-375" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-375">&#91;374&#93;</a></sup> Lamar has been outspoken about his faith in his music and interviews.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_373-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-373">&#91;372&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-376" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-376">&#91;375&#93;</a></sup> The introductory lines to <i>Good Kid, M.A.A.D City</i> includes a form of the <a href="/wiki/Sinner%27s_prayer" title="Sinner&#39;s prayer">Sinner's prayer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-377" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-377">&#91;376&#93;</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/I_(Kendrick_Lamar_song)" title="I (Kendrick Lamar song)">I</a>" (2014) and <i><a href="/wiki/Damn_(Kendrick_Lamar_album)" title="Damn (Kendrick Lamar album)">Damn</a></i> (2017) further discusses his Christian faith and spiritual struggles.<sup id="cite_ref-378" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-378">&#91;377&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-379" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-379">&#91;378&#93;</a></sup> Lamar credits <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> for his fame and "deliverance" from the crime he experienced in Compton.<sup id="cite_ref-380" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-380">&#91;379&#93;</a></sup> He believes his career is <a href="/wiki/Divine_inspiration" title="Divine inspiration">divinely inspired</a>, and that "God put something in my heart to get across and that's what I'm going to focus on, using my voice as an instrument and doing what needs to be done."<sup id="cite_ref-381" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-381">&#91;380&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For Halloween in 2014, Lamar dressed up as <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus Christ</a>. He explained his costume in an interview with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fader" title="The Fader">The Fader</a></i>, saying: "If I want to idolize somebody, I'm not going to do a scary monster, I'm not gonna do another artist or a human being—I'm gonna idolize the Master, who I feel is the Master, and try to walk in His light."<sup id="cite_ref-382" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-382">&#91;381&#93;</a></sup> On the cover of <i>Mr. Morale &amp; the Big Steppers</i> (2022), and during select promotions for the album, Lamar wore a diamond <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_thorns" title="Crown of thorns">crown of thorns</a> designed by <a href="/wiki/Tiffany_%26_Co." title="Tiffany &amp; Co.">Tiffany &amp; Co.</a> He later explained that he wore the crown as a nod of respect to the artists who came before him, a reminder of his imperfections, and as a representation of "one of the greatest <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophets</a> that ever walked the Earth."<sup id="cite_ref-383" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-383">&#91;382&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philanthropy_and_activism">Philanthropy and activism</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Kendrick_Lamar_Obama_Janelle_Mon%C3%A1e_2016.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Kendrick_Lamar_Obama_Janelle_Mon%C3%A1e_2016.jpg/220px-Kendrick_Lamar_Obama_Janelle_Mon%C3%A1e_2016.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Kendrick_Lamar_Obama_Janelle_Mon%C3%A1e_2016.jpg/330px-Kendrick_Lamar_Obama_Janelle_Mon%C3%A1e_2016.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Kendrick_Lamar_Obama_Janelle_Mon%C3%A1e_2016.jpg/440px-Kendrick_Lamar_Obama_Janelle_Mon%C3%A1e_2016.jpg 2x" data-file-width="950" data-file-height="643" /></a><figcaption>Lamar (left) at the 2016 <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(United_States)" title="Independence Day (United States)">Independence Day</a> celebration with President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> (center) and singer <a href="/wiki/Janelle_Mon%C3%A1e" title="Janelle Monáe">Janelle Monáe</a> (right)</figcaption></figure><p>In November 2011, Lamar headlined a <a href="/wiki/Benefit_concert" title="Benefit concert">charity concert</a> benefitting Los Angeles' Downtown Women's Center, which addresses the needs of women battling homelessness and poverty in <a href="/wiki/Skid_Row,_Los_Angeles" title="Skid Row, Los Angeles">Skid Row</a>, and Project RISHI, a non-profit organization fighting poverty in villages across <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-384" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-384">&#91;383&#93;</a></sup> He voiced his support for <a href="/wiki/Frank_Ocean" title="Frank Ocean">Frank Ocean</a> after he <a href="/wiki/Coming_out" title="Coming out">came out</a> via an <a href="/wiki/Open_letter" title="Open letter">open letter</a>, and criticized rappers who negatively reacted to the letter yet wear luxury clothing created by <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT+</a> designers.<sup id="cite_ref-385" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-385">&#91;384&#93;</a></sup> He donated to the <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">American Red Cross</a> in November 2012 to support victims of <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Sandy" title="Hurricane Sandy">Hurricane Sandy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-386" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-386">&#91;385&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the midst of the <a href="/wiki/2012_United_States_presidential_election" title="2012 United States presidential election">2012 U.S. presidential election</a>, Lamar stated that he doesn’t vote because "everything has a contradiction, everything is higher ranking and way beyond us, way beyond people."<sup id="cite_ref-387" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-387">&#91;386&#93;</a></sup> He continued by noting, "When I say the president can't even control the world, then you definitely know there's something else out there pushing the buttons. They could do whatever they want to, we['re] all puppets."<sup id="cite_ref-388" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-388">&#91;387&#93;</a></sup> Lamar retracted his statements several days before the election following swift backlash. He told <a href="/wiki/MTV_News" title="MTV News">MTV News</a> in November, "I think we just really need to take matters into our own hands, uplift our community, put money back in our community and show these kids how there's something different". He also shared that he would be voting for President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> because he believes his opponent, <a href="/wiki/Mitt_Romney" title="Mitt Romney">Mitt Romney</a>, doesn't have "a good heart at all."<sup id="cite_ref-389" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-389">&#91;388&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2013, Lamar donated $50,000 to Centennial High School's music department.<sup id="cite_ref-390" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-390">&#91;389&#93;</a></sup> He embarked on a five-stop tour in 2014, with all proceeds benefitting <a href="/wiki/Habitat_for_Humanity" title="Habitat for Humanity">Habitat for Humanity</a> and his hometown of Compton.<sup id="cite_ref-391" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-391">&#91;390&#93;</a></sup> Lamar is a vocal advocate for the <a href="/wiki/Black_Lives_Matter" title="Black Lives Matter">Black Lives Matter</a> movement, and "<a href="/wiki/Alright_(Kendrick_Lamar_song)" title="Alright (Kendrick Lamar song)">Alright</a>" serves as the movement's unofficial anthem.<sup id="cite_ref-392" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-392">&#91;391&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-393" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-393">&#91;392&#93;</a></sup> He has argued that the song is the "biggest record in the world" because of how it impacted others: "You might not have heard it on the radio all day, but you're seeing it on the news, and you're seeing it in communities, and people felt it."<sup id="cite_ref-394" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-394">&#91;393&#93;</a></sup> "Alright", and its performance at the <a href="/wiki/BET_Awards_2015" title="BET Awards 2015">2015 BET Awards</a>, was heavily criticized by <a href="/wiki/Fox_News" title="Fox News">Fox News</a> and conservative journalist <a href="/wiki/Geraldo_Rivera" title="Geraldo Rivera">Geraldo Rivera</a>, who claimed it sends "exactly the wrong message" and is "why I say that hip hop has done more damage to young African Americans than racism in recent years."<sup id="cite_ref-395" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-395">&#91;394&#93;</a></sup> Responding to his remarks, Lamar stressed that "hip hop is not the problem, our reality is the problem of the situation. [...] Rather [than] going out here and doing the murders myself, I want to express myself in a positive light the same way other artists are doing."<sup id="cite_ref-396" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-396">&#91;395&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-397" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-397">&#91;396&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lamar met President Obama for the first time in October 2015 at the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>, where they discussed "topics concerning the inner cities, the problem, the solutions and furthermore embracing the youth, both being aware that mentoring saves lives." Prior to their conversation, Obama shared that his favorite song of the year was Lamar's "How Much a Dollar Cost".<sup id="cite_ref-398" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-398">&#91;397&#93;</a></sup> The meeting was documented in a <a href="/wiki/Public_service_announcement" title="Public service announcement">public service announcement</a> video in support of <a href="/wiki/MENTOR" title="MENTOR">MENTOR: The National Mentoring Partnership</a> and Obama's <a href="/wiki/My_Brother%27s_Keeper_Challenge" title="My Brother&#39;s Keeper Challenge">My Brother's Keeper Challenge</a>, which addresses the barriers young <a href="/wiki/Person_of_color" title="Person of color">men of color</a> face and promotes intervention by civic leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-399" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-399">&#91;398&#93;</a></sup> Lamar shared in a cover story with <i>Billboard</i> that he looked to Obama in the same light that "people look at me these days," before adding, "We tend to forget that people who've attained a certain position are human."<sup id="cite_ref-400" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-400">&#91;399&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite <i>Damn</i> (2017) being conceived during the <a href="/wiki/2016_United_States_presidential_election" title="2016 United States presidential election">2016 U.S. presidential election</a>, and released in the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>'s presidency, Lamar barely spoke about Trump on the album. He believes persistent remarks about him is "like <a href="/wiki/Flogging_a_dead_horse" title="Flogging a dead horse">beating a dead horse</a>. We already know what it is. Are we gonna keep talking about it or are we gonna take action?"<sup id="cite_ref-401" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-401">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup> Lamar did call Trump's election a "complete mindfuck" while speaking to <a href="/wiki/Tour%C3%A9_(journalist)" title="Touré (journalist)">Touré</a> for <i><a href="/wiki/I-D" title="I-D">I-D</a></i>, and explained that the key differences between him and Obama are "morals, dignity, principles, [and] common sense. How can you follow someone who doesn't know how to approach someone or speak to them kindly and with compassion and sensitivity?"<sup id="cite_ref-402" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-402">&#91;401&#93;</a></sup> In July 2017, Lamar purchased a wheelchair-accessible van for his <a href="/wiki/Tetraplegia" title="Tetraplegia">quadriplegic</a> fan, Jennifer Phillips.<sup id="cite_ref-403" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-403">&#91;402&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-404" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-404">&#91;403&#93;</a></sup> In September, he performed at Rihanna's third annual Diamond Ball in support of her <a href="/wiki/Clara_Lionel_Foundation" title="Clara Lionel Foundation">Clara Lionel Foundation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-405" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-405">&#91;404&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-406" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-406">&#91;405&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lamar has regularly performed at TDE’s annual holiday toy drive at <a href="/wiki/Nickerson_Gardens" title="Nickerson Gardens">Nickerson Gardens</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-407" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-407">&#91;406&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-408" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-408">&#91;407&#93;</a></sup> and organizes his own toy drive in Compton.<sup id="cite_ref-409" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-409">&#91;408&#93;</a></sup> In June 2020, he joined a Compton <a href="/wiki/Peace_walk" title="Peace walk">peace walk</a> to protest against the murders of <a href="/wiki/George_Floyd" title="George Floyd">George Floyd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Breonna_Taylor" title="Breonna Taylor">Breonna Taylor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-410" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-410">&#91;409&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-411" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-411">&#91;410&#93;</a></sup> As he concluded his performance at <a href="/wiki/Glastonbury_Festival_2022" title="Glastonbury Festival 2022">Glastonbury Festival</a> in June 2022, Lamar chanted "They judged you, they judged Christ. Godspeed for women's rights" in response to the <a href="/wiki/Dobbs_v._Jackson_Women%27s_Health_Organization" title="Dobbs v. Jackson Women&#39;s Health Organization">overturning of <i>Roe v. Wade</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-412" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-412">&#91;411&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
